0061452068|4.0|I don't like this book. I don't like it at all.<br /><br />So why did I give it four stars? It's because the book starts out well, and is useful but eventually it declines into a polemic for creative, compassionate, conservatism. I like the idea of books that tell you the world is not coming to an end, and there are many reasons which are raised here that support that point of view. All good stuff, and things that I believe in.<br /><br />But where the book goes wrong is past the half way mark. I can accept Matt Ridley's points that civilization was helped by the notion of barter. I think there are other points of view about barter but it is a reasonable attitude. Where Ridley comes unstuck is when he moves to present-day concerns about the planet.<br /><br />It seems to him that the current set up of states is sufficient to keep the world going. I think that Matt has a more open point of view than some people on the right about climate change. However the intimation is that all will be well if we let the same people run the world in the same way. I don't believe that forthcoming climate change will be fatal, but I do believe it needs attention.<br /><br />It's very easy to rebut the arguments on climate change by pointing out some of the outlandish claims from the 1960s and 70s. It's rather like the man in the 1880s who believed that by 1920 main streets in cities would be impossible because of the huge amounts of horse poop. Things change. They get better for a number of reasons and the workings of the market are only a small part of the changes.<br /><br />Ridley does not take into account the skeptical nature of people. What we heard was that things like overpopulation and global warming would bring about major changes. What we thought was that something ought to be done, but it probably wasn't as bad as some were making out. And each warning was based on scientific research that showed that there was some problem and that we needed to watch it carefully.<br /><br />These ideas have entered most people's thinking. Maybe some things about Rachel Carson's \\"Silent Spring\\" were overstated, but we've taken steps to make sure that we haven't carried on with the bad practices which she drew our attention to. And many of the new rules are ones that impact a vast majority. You can't take your groceries home in a plastic bag anymore. You're encouraged to use recycling schemes set up by local authorities everywhere.<br /><br />Most people are aware that there are environmental processes that need to be carried out. That applies just as much to people who want to have coffee in non-biodegradable cups and also businesses who want to discharge waste into the water supply. That means the cost of doing business goes up but so does the cost of living. The environment affects us all.<br /><br />There is probably a point somewhere in this book where Ridley could have finished and given us a book well worth reading. Maybe you'll notice it, maybe you won't. So I advise you to read it with a critical eye. For lots of the pages, you won't need to bother too much. It's worth the read, but if you want a better introduction to the same subject, try the book \\"Abundance.\\" It doesn't come with the same historical perspective that Ridley's book does, but it will reassure you that the world is not going to hell in a hand basket.br />These ideas have entered most people's thinking. Its main purpose is to make the characters and their plot-lines familiar to the readers, so that they can easily dive in this fantasy universe that is so special, unique and simply delicious that it's impossible not to become addictive.<br /><br />The first conclusion after reading A Game of Thrones is that it's useless trying to summarize it. There are so many characters involved and they all have their background stories, they all belong to a major or minor house in The Seven Kingdoms and they all pursue their own goals. The story is told from the point of view of eight main characters, thus the plot-lines developing in parallel, in different parts of Martin's fantasy world: Lord Eddard Stark, Lady Catelyn Stark, Sansa Stark, Arya Stark, Bran Stark, Jon Snow, Tyrion Lannister and Daenerys Targaryen. There are three major plot-lines according to the locations where the action takes place: in the Seven Kingdoms, on the Wall and across the Narrow Sea.<br /><br />In the Seven Kingdoms, King Robert Baratheon comes in the north to ask Eddard Stark, Lord of Winterfell, to be the Hand of the King. Though he loathes leaving his peaceful life and beautiful family behind, Lord Stark has to obey his king and go in the south with his two daughters, Sansa and Arya, even when his youngest son mysteriously falls off a high tower and goes into a deep sleep. His wife, Catelyn, is left with three sons: Robb, the eldest, Bran, who is invalid, and Rickon. Jon Snow, Eddard Stark's bastard son, decides to join the Night's Watch to protect the realm from the wild people and creatures beyond the Wall. And this is, basically, how a game of cat and mouse between two major houses (Stark and Lannister) begins, the first trying to protect a useless king and the other trying to take his throne.<br /><br />All the while, in the East, across the Narrow Sea, Viserys Targaryen does everything in his power to raise an army and take back what's rightfully his, the Seven Kingdoms, for he is the true heir of the Iron Throne. He sells his own sister, Daenerys, to a Dothraki warlord, Khal Drogo, in exchange for the army he needs. But he makes mistake after mistake and Daenerys proves to be smarter than him. Also, she's got three petrified dragon eggs. Are they just useless stones or do dragons still exist?<br /><br />The Night's Watch is the only one that doesn't care about kings and thrones. Its purpose is to protect Westeros of what lies beyond the Wall and rumors say that they have to fear more than just some tribes of wild, ragged people. There are these creatures, which legends call Others, that bring cold, smell like pure, savage winter, have sparkling blue eyes and can only be killed by fire.<br />All in all, A Game of Thrones is an amazing must-read that ends in such a cliffhanger that you have to grab the next book in the series A Song of Ice and Fire - A Clash of Kings. George R.R. Martin succeeded in creating an addictive universe with strong, determined characters that are so complex you come to the conclusion that there might just not be any bad characters, but just some people who're trying to outsmart their rivals.rything in his power to raise an army and take back what's rightfully his, the Seven Kingdoms, for he is the true heir of the Iron Throne. 0812567110|4.0|The Picture of Dorian Gray is not just a book, it is a story that delves into each human's beliefs and views of the world, a book that makes a person really look differently at the world around them. Although Oscar Wilde wastes much space with over-explanatory details, the moments in which he tells his story are moments of inspiration and causes of great self-examination. The story, in which a good-looking young man gains eternal beauty, really makes its reader look within themselves and find the person they are and who they want to be. Also, through some very good symbols, Wilde is able to make his reader find new meaning in the world around them and is able to open the reader's eyes to the magnificence--and ugliness--of life itself.<br /> Even though the book is mostly filled with simple dialogue and extended (and sometimes, unnecessary) descriptions, the overall effect the story itself can have upon the reader's mind is overpowering. Not only does Wilde's occasional complex dialogue add to the reader's understanding of the world, the ideals with which Wilde writes (and therefore gives his character) make the reader work to understand where they fall within the spectrum of day to day life.<br /> The book was slow moving at first, but really started to pick up, due to its author's ability to excite the reader's mind, with the first meeting between the main character and the man who he would eventually blame for all of his problems. I would definitely recommend this book to those who have an open mind (there are some controversial ideas and lifestyles within the story) and are able to follow a character's complex mindset because, although most of the book is simple and in some people's eyes, artless, it still provides the reader with multiple \\"out-of-the-box\\" type ideas.<br />  I enjoyed the book, and the message it sent was one that I will probably never forget. It is one that forces the reader to question what they value in life, make them question what a \\"perfect\\" life would be. Because of the story's ability to reach within the readers mind and plant a new outlook on life, the book would be a great read for anyone who is interested in hearing the story of a man who gave up everything in order to keep the only thing he believed to be important within the world, the thing that he himself feared losing the most.\\" life would be. 1451648537|5.0|Isaacson has written an outstanding biography of this most iconic figure in modern American technology.  The research, including many interviews with Jobs [who sanctioned Isaacson to write this, and cooperated] and nearly everyone who knew or worked with him, is clearly meticulous and thorough. He doesn't sugar-coat Jobs' irascible behavior or his weird idiosyncrasies which makes the story all the more powerful.  The book is about 100 pages too long due to Isaacson's tendency to repeat himself on some points but that is a small complaint.<br /><br />I will say I wish there had been a bit more about the loved ones in his life: his adoptive parents drop out of the picture early on even though Jobs loved them dearly and was inspired to his perfectionism at least partially by his adoptive father whom he respected and revered; likewise, we learn little about his wife other than her inability to cajole him into doing things he didn't want to do; his adoptive sister  is mentioned only once or twice; his relationship with his children is analyzed but not enough to give a true picture.  Finally, I wish there had been more about his travels and true friendships and how that influenced  him [or not].<br /><br />At its core, this is an elegy and as such, it is, in a way, a sad tale.
